<div class="readable stacked" id="description" style="right:0">
<span id="freeTextContainer17044302888050649793"><strong>
<em>'Liberty, equality, fraternity, or death; -- the last, much the easiest to bestow, O Guillotine!'</em>
</strong><br><br>After eighteen years as a political prisoner in the Bastille, the ageing Doctor Manette is finally released and reunited with his daughter in England. There the lives of two very different men, Charles Darnay, an exiled French aristocrat, and Sydney Carton, a disreputable but</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText17044302888050649793" style="display:none"><strong>
<em>'Liberty, equality, fraternity, or death; -- the last, much the easiest to bestow, O Guillotine!'</em>
</strong><br><br>After eighteen years as a political prisoner in the Bastille, the ageing Doctor Manette is finally released and reunited with his daughter in England. There the lives of two very different men, Charles Darnay, an exiled French aristocrat, and Sydney Carton, a disreputable but brilliant English lawyer, become enmeshed through their love for Lucie Manette. From the tranquil roads of London, they are drawn against their will to the vengeful, bloodstained streets of Paris at the height of the Reign of Terror, and they soon fall under the lethal shadow of La Guillotine.<br><br>This edition uses the text as it appeared in its serial publication in 1859 to convey the full scope of Dickens's vision, and includes the original illustrations by H. K. Browne ('Phiz'). Richard Maxwell's introduction discusses the intricate interweaving of epic drama with personal tragedy.</br></br></br></br></span>

<div class="communityQuestionAndAnswer">
<div class="communityQuestion">
<div class="questionText">
<a href="/questions/316361-i-just-tried-this-book-yesterday-and">I just tried this book yesterday, and barely came trough the first 30 pages. I didnt exactly know who said what, and what there was said. Is it true that this book may be quite a work to read? That i just should try again in som years? Or do you have any tips on how to read this book in a nice way? :)</a>
</div>
<div class="communityQAFooter questionFooter">
<ul class="questionFooterActions">
<li class="footerItem like">
<span class="likeItContainer" id="like_container_community_question_316361"><a href="/rating/voters/316361?resource_type=CommunityQuestion" id="like_count_community_question_316361" rel="nofollow"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F1953.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" rel="nofollow">like</a></span>
</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp">
<a href="/questions/316361-i-just-tried-this-book-yesterday-and">36 days ago</a>
</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionPermalink">
<a href="/questions/316361-i-just-tried-this-book-yesterday-and">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
</div>
<div class="allCommunityAnswers">
<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/31278892-sarah"><img alt="" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/100x100/crop/users/1421729325/31278892.jpg"/></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/31278892-sarah">Sarah</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent"><em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is definitely a challenging novel, so please don't feel discouraged if you're not getting as much out of it as you hoped! It is<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent"><em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is definitely a challenging novel, so please don't feel discouraged if you're not getting as much out of it as you hoped! It is true that the novel takes a bit of thought and, for lack of a better word, work to get through. But for all the obstacles, I do believe that it was worth every painstaking second!<br><br>The best advice I can give you are as follows:<br>1. Download a dictionary app on your mobile and keep it near you while you read. It's a quick way to improve comprehension. And Dickens really loved his vocab words!<br>2. If you ever come across a sentence or paragraph which you can't quite understand, the best thing to do is read it one (or two) more times, and just move on. If you can just get the general gist of what Dickens is trying to say, then you're doing great! Try not to get too hung up on parts you don't understand.<br>3. If there is a point in the <em>plot</em> which is a bit confusing, briefly skim over the section once, lock that moment in your mind, and move on. <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is like a drama. The first half is the set up, and may be crazy confusing, but everything comes together in the end to paint one amazing picture.<br>4. Try going to summary sites like litcharts or sparknotes. I really recommend litcharts, which gives you a detailed summary of what happened in the novel, and also gives analyses. Here's the site: <a href="http://www.litcharts.com/lit/a-tale-of-two-cities" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">http://www.litcharts.com/lit/a-tale-o...</a><br>5. Buy an annotated edition of <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em>. If you get the Barnes and Nobles edition, they'll give you footnotes and endnotes, which really clarify the historical and political situation. I'm not quite sure of Penguin Classics' endnotes, since I stick to B&amp;N, but both companies are very good.<br><br>Again, this is a really challenging book. I really hope you decide to push on with Dickens, because he is worth it at the end! But I also completely understand if you want to take a break and lay the novel off to the side for a bit. There's nothing wrong with taking a break from a book!<br><br>Best wishes~<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

          $$('div.prototip')[0].setStyle({zIndex: 6000});
        }
      });
      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script> <script type="text/javascript">
      var $ratingGraph = $j('#rating_graph');
      var $copy;
      if ($ratingGraph.length > 0) {
          $copy = $ratingGraph.clone();
          $j('#rating_details_tip_graph').remove();
          $copy.attr('id', 'rating_details_tip_graph');
          $copy.find('script').remove();
          $j('#rating_details_tip').prepend($copy);
      }
  </script>

<div class="section firstReview">
<div class="review" id="review_13354511" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13354511" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder" title="Melissa Rudder"><img alt="Melissa Rudder" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1201213962p2/824216.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13354511?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">May 29, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder" title="Melissa Rudder">Melissa Rudder</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/824216-melissa-rudder?shelf=teach-it">teach-it</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer13354511" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer2189783639662072073">My primary goal when I'm teaching <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> to my sophomores is to make them realize that Charles Dickens didn't write creaky, dusty long novels that teachers embraced as a twisted rite of passage for teenagers.  Instead, I want them them to understand why Dickens was <s>one of</s> the most popular writer<s>s</s> in England and America during his time.  I want them to see the book as the suspenseful, comedic, and sentimental piece of entertainment that it is.  Because, while <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is ma</span>
<span id="freeText2189783639662072073" style="display:none">My primary goal when I'm teaching <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> to my sophomores is to make them realize that Charles Dickens didn't write creaky, dusty long novels that teachers embraced as a twisted rite of passage for teenagers.  Instead, I want them them to understand why Dickens was <s>one of</s> the most popular writer<s>s</s> in England and America during his time.  I want them to see the book as the suspenseful, comedic, and sentimental piece of entertainment that it is.  Because, while <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is masterfully written with sly humor, densely meaningful descriptions, a cast of quirky characters only Dickens could create, an endless series of telling binaries and foils, and relevant social commentary about the French Revolution as well as Dickens' time, it is also simply a damn good story.  By a damn good storyteller.  <br><br>I have a difficult time writing reviews about books that I adore because, when I'm not reading them, I hug them too closely to be very critical.  (BTW - I frequently hug <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> in front of my students... and write Charles Dickens' name with hearts around it...  They think I'm crazy, but it intrigues some of them just enough to make them doubt the derisive comments of upperclassmen.)  I reluctantly admit that Dickens does oversimplify the causes of the French Revolution and the Reign of Terror; however, in doing so, he successfully captures the spirit of a tumultuous period and helps readers sympathize with characters on every side of the developing conflict.  I also think that the characters of Roger Cly and John Barsad get a bit messy and may have worked better as a single character.  Perhaps the confusion is a result of serialization restructuring.  But, really, I read <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> like a costumed <em>Lord of the Rings</em> fan at a movie premier.  I cheer when my favorite characters enter scenes and I knowingly laugh when Dickens cleverly foreshadows future events. <br><br>Though I don't think that <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is Dickens' best novel--that title I would reserve for either <em>Bleak House</em> or <em>David Copperfield</em>--I do agree with Dickens, who claims that it was his best story.  It is artfully written.  Dickens introduces a cast of characters, sprawled across two nations and spanning varied social classes and political affiliations, and then effortlessly weaves their stories and secrets together in a masterful way.  The Modernist movement painstakingly forced literature to reflect the ambiguities and uncertainties of the real world and that's great, but sometimes it is a real joy to read a story that ends with such magnificent closure.  All mysteries are solved and everything makes sense.  It is beautiful.<br><br>(I have to admit that I was overjoyed when a group of my fifth period girls persistently voiced their disdain for Dickens' angel in the house Lucie and backed Madame Defarge.  I think they may have created a Madame Defarge myspace, actually.  Oh how the times have changed.)<br><br>"Ms. R--, you got me." "What?" "At the beginning of this book, you said you would get some of us.  And that we would love it.  You got me."  I didn't get you G--.  Charles Dickens did.  I just introduced you.<br><br>Quote:<br><br>"A wonderful fact to reflect upon, that every human creature is constituted to be that profound secret and mystery to every other."</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="2189783639662072073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_318853290" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/318853290" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7455393-emma" title="Emma"><img alt="Emma" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1398422247p2/7455393.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/318853290?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 13, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/7455393-emma" title="Emma">Emma</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7455393-emma?shelf=classics">classics</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer318853290" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer15880825617217172837">Christ on a bike - I’d forgotten how much concentration Dickens demands. <br><br>Reading the first few chapters of this book was, frankly, a chore.  I could not be less bothered about The Mail and the more Dickens banged on about that never ending carriage journey the more I daydreamed about the next book I was going to read once this torture was over. I’m glad I didn’t give up though because as soon as we hit France and the wine shop I was hooked, the pace started to pick up and there were mysteries and</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText15880825617217172837" style="display:none">Christ on a bike - I’d forgotten how much concentration Dickens demands.  <br> <br>Reading the first few chapters of this book was, frankly, a chore.  I could not be less bothered about The Mail and the more Dickens banged on about that never ending carriage journey the more I daydreamed about the next book I was going to read once this torture was over. I’m glad I didn’t give up though because as soon as we hit France and the wine shop I was hooked, the pace started to pick up and there were mysteries and revelations galore.<br> <br>There is so much in this book.  It would take me a month to provide anything other than a quick and dirty overview - which I can't really do either. Just think London/revolutionary France 1775, unrequited love, revenge, a doppelgänger and la guillotine.<br> <br>I loved the gothic feel to the book, Jerry Crunchers body snatching, the remote settings, the macabre events, the parts of the book that gave me an uneasy feeling.  Right up my street.<br> <br>I fell for Sidney Carton pretty much straight away too.  Bohemian, brilliant, indifferent.   J'adore Sidney Carton.  Although we never find out exactly what is up with Sid; I wonder what would have become of him and how I would have viewed him had he not become a hero. A few main characters were also a little too underdeveloped for me to connect with them.  Lucie Manette left me feeling ‘meh’.  She links nearly every character in the book and inspires love in seemingly every direction but whilst likable enough, there is no depth and certainly not my type of heroine.  But, I guess this was written in 1859 and Lucie is, I suppose, the type of heroine that would appeal to the readers of the time. <br> <br>Madame Defarge, the antithesis of Lucie, on the other hand is marvellous.  Clearly the villain of the piece, cold, consumed by revenge and not really even human by the end of the book but still, enthralling stuff. I think Dickens achieves a good balance with the historical telling of the revolution but perhaps was a little unfair to Madame Defarge, her motives and back story being revealed far too late in the book. <br><br>Overall though, I got an awful lot out of a Tale of Two Cities; I'm still heartbroken over Sidney's final thoughts and his vision of a better Paris <em>'fair to look upon, with not a trace of this day's disfigurement'</em>; The defarges, with their Macbeth and Lady Macbeth qualities <em>'infirm of purpose, give me the daggers'</em> - possibly the greatest dickens characters I've ever read - were awful and captivating; The oppression of the peasants, their plight and the awfulness of the revolution carefully told with historical accuracy.  The only reason a Tale of Two Cities didn't get five stars is because of that bloody awful carriage ride. <br> </br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="15880825617217172837" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_14982020" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14982020" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/891206-leslie" title="Leslie"><img alt="Leslie" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301450802p2/891206.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14982020?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 02, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/891206-leslie" title="Leslie">Leslie</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer14982020'); return false;">click here.</a>
</em>
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14982020" style="display:none">
<span id="freeTextContainer9179304965159973630">Most satisfying ending in the English language.  <br><br>Yes, the last line is a classic ("It is a far, far better thing ..."), concluding, in astonishingly concise language (for Dickens), the peace and redemption of the story's most poignant romantic hero.  But this novel delivers such a gratifying experience because there are, in fact, many characters who cover significant emotional ground in their journey to love one woman as best they can.  <br><br>Lucie's father battles his way back from madness under the gen</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText9179304965159973630" style="display:none">Most satisfying ending in the English language.  <br><br>Yes, the last line is a classic ("It is a far, far better thing ..."), concluding, in astonishingly concise language (for Dickens), the peace and redemption of the story's most poignant romantic hero.  But this novel delivers such a gratifying experience because there are, in fact, many characters who cover significant emotional ground in their journey to love one woman as best they can.  <br><br>Lucie's father battles his way back from madness under the gentle protection of his daughter.  Lucie's childhood nursemaid evolves from a comical stereotype to an embattled force to be reckoned with.  Lucie's husband's well-meaning (if bland) <em>
<a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/153525.noblesse_oblige" title="noblesse oblige by Nancy Mitford">noblesse oblige</a>
</em> culminates in -- not his hoped-for heroic moment, but a moment of quiet dignity that is most moving for its humility.  Even Lucie's <em>banker</em> reaches dizzying heights of heroic accomplishment when Dickens appoints the quiet businessman the vehicle for an entire family's escape from the guillotine.<br><br>It is true that Lucie herself engages the reader less than her brutal counterpart -- the broken but terrifying Madame Defarge -- is able to, as modern readers are less moved by  the swooning heroines who populate the period's "literature of sensibility." But we can certainly respond to Dickens' powerful and vivid claim: love is not only what makes us human, it is what allows us to be, at times, superhuman.<br><br>And when Sydney Carton, in equal parts love and despair, tells Lucie that "there is a man who would give his life to keep a life you love beside you" ... ? <br><br>I go to pieces. Every damn time.<br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="9179304965159973630" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="xhr_comment_body">
<a href="/user/show/27221082-janet-doyle" style="font-weight:bold">Janet Doyle</a>
<span id="freeTextContainer11598098204752250741">I can understand how modern females might find Lucy rather cloying because today we admire strength, independence and a feisty spirit in women. Today'</span>
<span id="freeText11598098204752250741" style="display:none">I can understand how modern females might find Lucy rather cloying because today we admire strength, independence and a feisty spirit in women. Today's heroines are often the forces of action in a story, rather than people to whom things happen.. However, I think she shows strength of character in devotion to first her father and then her husband. She remains strong for them no matter what the circumstances. This strength takes integrity and real love and that is never old-fashioned, Dickens describes her as the"golden thread" that binds them. Modern women should not dismiss that idea of binding family and friends through the power of caring. It is a very real force.</span>

                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040930-laura?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040930-laura?shelf=perennial-favorite">perennial-favorite</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040930-laura?shelf=english-lit">english-lit</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer24343601" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer5125161524598842110">Years of teaching this novel to teenagers never dimmed my thrill in reading it — if anything, I grew to love it more every time I watched kids gasp aloud at the revelations! Critics are divided on its place in the Dickens canon, but the ones who think it an inferior work are simply deranged. It has everything: dark deeds, revolution, madness, love, thwarted love, forgiveness, revenge, and a stunning act of self-sacrifice. And melodrama! Oh, how Dickens loved melodrama, but in <em>A Tale of Two Citie</em></span>
<span id="freeText5125161524598842110" style="display:none">Years of teaching this novel to teenagers never dimmed my thrill in reading it — if anything, I grew to love it more every time I watched kids gasp aloud at the revelations! Critics are divided on its place in the Dickens canon, but the ones who think it an inferior work are simply deranged. It has everything: dark deeds, revolution, madness, love, thwarted love, forgiveness, revenge, and a stunning act of self-sacrifice. And melodrama! Oh, how Dickens loved melodrama, but in <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> it reaches truly grand proportions. <br><br>It’s the ultimate mystery novel: characters act strangely, but always for a reason. Miscellaneous people drift in and out, but they’re not truly miscellaneous — you just have to wait to see how they’re connected. And like any good mystery, the payoff at the end is worth the time it takes to get there...and what a payoff! Dickens is a master of the type of narration that simultaneously moves forward and back in time. In other words, strategically placed revelations from the past inform the present and shape the future. The brilliant timing both of his hints and of the actual revelations is a bonus field of study. Merely the drama of the dark past and its impact on the “here and now” story is thrilling enough. Plus, <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is a profoundly moral story, with themes of vengeance versus forgiveness, sins of the fathers being visited on the children, resurrection and rebirth, and the possibility of redemption. <br/></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="5125161524598842110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38425096?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 04, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu" title="Jason Koivu">Jason Koivu</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer38425096" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer17198638656004305705">Hands down my favorite Dickens' I've read yet! It's got love, sacrifice, revenge, revolt and other exciting verbs! I'm a big fan of a solid marriage between character development and action. <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is well-wed. Some criticize Dickens for his trite stories and overblown caricature-esque characters. Yes, the man wrote some less-than-perfect books. He wrote them for a wide-ranging public and he wrote for money. High-minded prose eloquently crafted may garner praise, but it doesn't alw</span>
<span id="freeText17198638656004305705" style="display:none">Hands down my favorite Dickens' I've read yet! It's got love, sacrifice, revenge, revolt and other exciting verbs! I'm a big fan of a solid marriage between character development and action. <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> is well-wed. Some criticize Dickens for his trite stories and overblown caricature-esque characters. Yes, the man wrote some less-than-perfect books. He wrote them for a wide-ranging public and he wrote for money. High-minded prose eloquently crafted may garner praise, but it doesn't always pay the bills. But here you get the author at his finest, plotting a riveting tale and creating sympathetic characters with empathy up the wazoo. The great descriptions of the rebellion are interesting, but it's the dual nature of the revolutionaries that I really love. Dickens makes you feel for their plight and then twists it around, so that the tortured become the tyrants and your fondness turns to loathing as you witness their despicable deeds. "Feel" is the operative word there. Dickens put a lot of feeling into <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em>. <br><br/></br></span>
<a data-text-id="17198638656004305705" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class="review" id="review_271690411" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271690411" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5008212-fahad" title="Fahad"><img alt="Fahad" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1384156960p2/5008212.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271690411?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 23, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/5008212-fahad" title="Fahad">Fahad</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/7026520" title="Hardcover 9953633266">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5008212-fahad?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5008212-fahad?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer271690411" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer762567059829833875">قصة مدينتين<br><br>   استعرت هذه الرواية من مكتبة الجامعة في بداية الألفية، كان ذلك قبل عالم الانترنت، عندما كنا لا نلتقي ولا نتعرف على الكتب ومشاهير المؤلفين إلا من خلال الصحف أو الكتب التي تسقط بين أيدينا اتفاقاً، ديكنز كان مألوفاً لي حينها، كنت قد قرأت له دايفد كوبرفيلد، وأعرف موقعه كروائي إنجليزي عظيم.<br><br>   حصلت على الكتاب الضخم، المغلف من قبل الجامعة بغلاف صلب، والمختوم مراراً كجواز سائح كوني، كنت غراً حينها، جديد على كل العوالم التي أمامي، فلذا حملت النسخة الضخمة محاولاً قراءتها خلال مهلة اليومين التي تم</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText762567059829833875" style="display:none">قصة مدينتين<br><br>   استعرت هذه الرواية من مكتبة الجامعة في بداية الألفية، كان ذلك قبل عالم الانترنت، عندما كنا لا نلتقي ولا نتعرف على الكتب ومشاهير المؤلفين إلا من خلال الصحف أو الكتب التي تسقط بين أيدينا اتفاقاً، ديكنز كان مألوفاً لي حينها، كنت قد قرأت له دايفد كوبرفيلد، وأعرف موقعه كروائي إنجليزي عظيم.<br><br>   حصلت على الكتاب الضخم، المغلف من قبل الجامعة بغلاف صلب، والمختوم مراراً كجواز سائح كوني، كنت غراً حينها، جديد على كل العوالم التي أمامي، فلذا حملت النسخة الضخمة محاولاً قراءتها خلال مهلة اليومين التي تمنحها الجامعة للكتب النادرة – قبل أن تبدأ الغرامات القاسية -، ولكن هذه المهمة كانت أكبر مني، فلذا اضطررت لإعادة الكتاب بعدما عبرت بداياته فقط، فيما بقيت صفحات طويلة وعدت نفسي بقراءتها يوماً ما.<br><br>   وجاء... ذلك الـ (يوماً ما) جاء، صحيح أنه تأخر قليلاً، ولكن لم يكن ذلك لأن يدي قصيرة عن الوصول إلى مدينتي ديكنز، وإنما لأن نهراً من الكتب جرفني من يومها، لقد تفتق العالم لي بعدها كما يتفتق لطفل قروي، لا يعرف أبعد من بيت أهله، ووجوه أهله، ثم يحمل ذات ليلة ليرمى في ميدان عاصمة، كل تلك الوجوه، كل تلك الألوان، الروائح، الناس الذاهبة والآيبة، كل تلك الأحداث، تربكه، تنزع توازنه، وفهمه لما حوله.<br><br>   وفي ذاكرتي، وعلى مر كل تلك السنوات، تداعت كلمات الكتاب وصوره ومشاهده، تحلل كل ما قرأته، بقي في ذاكرتي فقط وأنا أجذبه من رقدته بين مؤلفات كل أولئك الإنجليز العظماء، وصف مذهل لشارع قديم، كان ديكنز يأخذنا عبره، ليصعد بنا علية ما، حيث يقبع عجوز ما !! كان هذا كل ما بقي.<br><br>   عانى ديكنز في طفولته كثيراً، لم يتلق تعليماً جيداً، وحتى المدرسة المتواضعة التي ذهب إليها، سحب منها على عجل ليعمل لعشر ساعات يومياً، بعدما سجن والده لتراكم الديون عليه، والتحقت به والدته في السجن، وهو نظام غريب مطبق حينها !! هذه الأم ديكنز يشعر بأنها لا توليه العناية والاهتمام الكافيين، من هذه الظروف، ومن هذه المشاعر نلمس رؤية ديكنز ومواقفه تجاه الفقراء، وحقوق الأطفال، وتجاه المرأة.<br><br>   في هذه الرواية يبدو ديكنز مقارناً، بين مدينتين، باريس ولندن، نظامين ثوري وملكي، قضاءين ثوري ورسمي، وفي روايته التي كتبها مسلسلة، ونشرها في الصحف كما كان يفعل كتاب عصره، والتي لها سمات وميزات ذاك العصر وأدبه المليء بالأبطال الفروسيين، والنساء الجميلات المعشوقات من الجميع، والمصادفات التي تقبلها بصدر رحب لتستمتع، لتمضي قدماً.<br><br>   إنه عصر الثورة، تبدأ الأحداث قبل الثورة الفرنسية بقليل، حيث نتعرف على الدكتور مانيت، المسجون ظلماً في الباستيل ولسنوات طويلة – 18 عاماً -، والذي نتابع في الفصول الأولى لقائه بابنته لوسي والتي لم يكن يعلم بوجودها، وها هي تستنقذ والدها بمساعدة الثوري الفرنسي دوفارج وزوجته، وتأخذه إلى إنجلترا.<br><br>   بعد 5 سنوات يستعيد فيها الأب عقله، وتتزوج لوسي من تشارلز دارني، وهو نبيل فرنسي تخلى عن نبالته وذهب ليعيش في إنجلترا، تقوم الثورة في فرنسا، ويعرض لنا ديكنز حال الفرنسيين قبل الثورة وطريقة تعامل النبلاء معهم بأسلوب مذهل، ديكنز مذهل بحق في سرده، ساخر عظيم، لا ريب أن قراءه كانوا يتشوقون لكل فصل من فصول روايته.<br><br>   ترد تشارلز دارني وهو هناك في أمان إنجلترا، رسالة من خادم سابق له سجن في الباستيل، فيهرع إلى باريس لينقذه، فيقع بيد الثوريين ويقدم للمحاكمة والإعدام، تسرع لوسي ووالدها لاستنقاذه، خاصة والدكتور مانيت أحد نزلاء الباستيل المخضرمين، وهذا ما يكسبه الاحترام بين الثوار، هذا خلاف خبرته الطبية المفيدة لهم، وشخصيته العظيمة.<br><br>   تدور القصة، وتتشابك الأحداث ويلتقي ويتصارع الأبطال في تلك البقعة من باريس، وتنكشف الألغاز، وتقدم التضحيات، ويتركك ديكنز في النهاية وفي ذهنك وروحك ذلكم الشعور الملحمي الجميل.<br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="762567059829833875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class="review" id="review_90000799" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/90000799" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1639329-erik" title="Erik"><img alt="Erik" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1295747097p2/1639329.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/90000799?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 06, 2011</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1639329-erik" title="Erik">Erik</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/2907012-a-tale-of-two-cities" title="Audio CD 9781584723738">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1639329-erik?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1639329-erik?shelf=bottom-shelf">bottom-shelf</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer90000799" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer14914197884288582533">A Tale of Two Cities holds the dubious honor of being the first book I ever picked up and failed to finish.  The very first.<br><br>From there, it's all gone downhill.  Just look at my reviews where I casually admit to throwing away classics unread.  A Light in August, Lolita, The Elegance of the Hedgehog, etc, etc...<br><br>If you enjoy the little things, like being sane and not hating life, then I recommend you never pick this up.</br></br></br></br></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class="section">
<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_97512796" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/97512796" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2633899-dee" title="Dee"><img alt="Dee" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395804869p2/2633899.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/97512796?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 10, 2010</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2633899-dee" title="Dee">Dee</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/323612.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="Paperback 9780679729655">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2633899-dee?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2633899-dee?shelf=top-10-read-in-2010">top-10-read-in-2010</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2633899-dee?shelf=1001-books-list">1001-books-list</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2633899-dee?shelf=classic">classic</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer97512796" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer5683998237423233767">Picking up this book was a brave move. The only Dickens I'd ever managed to plod my way through was <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1258854.Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations by Charles Dickens">Great Expectations</a>. My expectations weren't great and unfortunately it didn't exceed them (probably been tainted by the film version with Gwyneth Paltrow where everything is green). I tried <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5088509.Hard_Times" title="Hard Times by Charles Dickens">Hard Times</a> and didn't get very far (a poor choice for a novice I'll bet--should have known from the title). I know the general gist of many of his other books and have intended to read them, but three days ago</span>
<span id="freeText5683998237423233767" style="display:none">Picking up this book was a brave move. The only Dickens I'd ever managed to plod my way through was <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1258854.Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations by Charles Dickens">Great Expectations</a>. My expectations weren't great and unfortunately it didn't exceed them (probably been tainted by the film version with Gwyneth Paltrow where everything is green). I tried <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5088509.Hard_Times" title="Hard Times by Charles Dickens">Hard Times</a> and didn't get very far (a poor choice for a novice I'll bet--should have known from the title). I know the general gist of many of his other books and have intended to read them, but three days ago I had <em>no</em> clue as to what <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1953.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="A Tale of Two Cities by Charles Dickens">A Tale of Two Cities</a> was about. I read on the back cover that Dickens himself believed it to be the greatest story he'd ever written, so, I figured if I tried and just couldn't do it then it would probably be over between me and him.<br><br>As you may have noticed from my rating I had a great one &amp; am pleased to say that I will now open the next Dickens on the list with (a little) eager expectation. Yes, there were long patches of dense prose, but they really weren't too bad. Probably because I find the content fascinating: the storming of the Bastille and the Terror. Horrific, both on the part of the nobility pre-Revolution and the citizens of the Republic. Incredibly well written and utterly engaging. I actually enjoyed reading the pages describing the times &amp; the imagery used to describe it. The 'sea of black and threatening waters' that rolled in wave after wave etc. And you've gotta love classic British dry wit &amp; satire (demonstrated perfectly in the first half of the chapter Monseigneur in Town).<br><br>A problem I have with many classics (with exceptions like <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7126.The_Count_of_Monte_Cristo" title="The Count of Monte Cristo by Alexandre Dumas">The Count of Monte Cristo</a> which I just could not put down) is that the pacing is all over the show. I was just not that interested in all the time spent in England riding on trains and getting married etc. The real story was in France with Madame &amp; Monsieur Defarge (or at least that's what I found the most interesting). To me the pace began to lag from about half way through until about the last 100 pages where it picked up dramatically. As soon as Sydney Carton came on the scene towards the end I could pick the outcome. Probably because the last line is so well known: 'It is a far, far better thing that I do, than I have ever done' etc. Nevertheless, I couldn't wait to see how it played out &amp; I wasn't disappointed. Yay!<br><br>So, I am pleased to say that I have made up with Dickens and now gladly welcome recommendations for the next best to read. Just maybe not Hard Times. ;)<br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="5683998237423233767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

<div class="section">
<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_175985056" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175985056" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat" title="Jan-Maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175985056?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 06, 2015</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat" title="Jan-Maat">Jan-Maat</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/567596.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="Paperback 9780140430547">review of another edition</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">People who aren't French and dislike 1789 and all that</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer175985056" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer592842448627984179">This book is interesting for the wrong reasons.  On the one hand there are elements that work very well and you feel confident in the author's skill but on the other hand the sequence of events that sucks one character after another back into France feels entirely unconvincing.<br><br>In <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31242.Bleak_House" title="Bleak House by Charles Dickens">Bleak House</a> we see a bundle of characteristics taken to a negative extreme in the person of the French women Hortense.  In <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> this is extended here to the point that 'bad' and 'French' seem to be synonym</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText592842448627984179" style="display:none">This book is interesting for the wrong reasons.  On the one hand there are elements that work very well and you feel confident in the author's skill but on the other hand the sequence of events that sucks one character after another back into France feels entirely unconvincing.<br><br>In <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31242.Bleak_House" title="Bleak House by Charles Dickens">Bleak House</a> we see a bundle of characteristics taken to a negative extreme in the person of the French women Hortense.  In <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> this is extended here to the point that 'bad' and 'French' seem to be synonymous terms as do 'good' and 'English'.  This culminates in the patriotic prize fight between the good English woman and the bad French woman.<br><br>Dickens wants to have his cake and eat it with his central plot conceit.  He shows the reader that the Ancien regime was bad and evil, that the people who suffered under it were innocent victims, but equally that the people who overthrew the Ancien Regime and seek revenge are also evil while at the same time getting round the notion that all French people are evil by finding all three of the good ones and lining them up in this story.  This isn't satisfying.  <br><br>In the context of early Victorian Britain it paid not to look too radical and there was a ready market for anti-French &amp; horrors of the French revolution stories.  This anti-French sentiment is executed so crudely that while I'm convinced and moved by Dickens' social commentary in <em>Hard Times</em>, <em>Bleak House</em> or <em>Great Expectations</em> I'm entirely mistrustful of what I'm being told in <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em>.<br><br>Politically the timing of this novel is interesting.  Dickens is writing eight years before the Second Reform Act, which extended the franchise in Britain, and he is looking back at the circumstances of the late eighteenth century.  In terms of the cruelty and hardships of the best of times and the worse of times there are similarities between the two countries.  Dickens is looking back over a subsequent period of slow and pragmatic reform in Britain and violent change in France.  The reason he gives implicitly in his text for the different political outcomes is purely in the character of the two nations.  The French are too passionate, while the phlegmatic English, and here remember Mr Rouncewell's 'Saxon' face in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31242.Bleak_House" title="Bleak House by Charles Dickens">Bleak House</a> contrasted with the aristocratic Dedlock, are constituently (politically and personally) capable of pragmatic change.<br><br>At once we see both political radicalism and a certain conservatism in Dickens' views.  On the one hand the stress on the Saxon compared with the Frenchman takes us back to the Norman Yoke ideology, a mainstay of English political radicalism from the Levellers to the Chartists, yet the implications are conservative.  Political life in Britain can change, pragmatically and effectively because of the nature of the English people.  The lack of the traits that allow the English to be fundamentally democratic in the national character of the French however condemns them to being an example to avoid.    <br><br>This is enormously reassuring to the comfortable classes subscribing to Dickens' periodicals.  Even the still distant threat on the horizon of extending the franchise to decent working men can be weathered due to inherent English pragmatism <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the product of our magnificent mucus no doubt as all affectionados of the four humours will agree <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.  The Saxon national character will prevent the violence of a French Revolution from occurring in Britain.<br><br>At the same time Dickens' personal life was anything but phlegmatic and pragmatic.  Perhaps something of the tension between the personal and the public gives the book the power it has.  The fight isn't between the good Englishwoman and the bad Frenchwoman, it is Dickens at war with himself.  Publicly pragmatism wins, personally, however, it is all about passion. <!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="592842448627984179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review" id="review_100184670" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100184670" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2638828-michael" title="Michael"><img alt="Michael" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1403308568p2/2638828.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100184670?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 27, 2010</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2638828-michael" title="Michael">Michael</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/17170.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="Hardcover 9781593083328">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2638828-michael?shelf=read-in-2010">read-in-2010</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2638828-michael?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2638828-michael?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer100184670" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer4963494073362155303">What a book! After reading this, I've come to appreciate Charles Dickens as so much more than "that guy who wrote the Christmas Carol."<br><br>One thing I love is his ability to create a perfect storyline. Everything in this book fits together in the end like a perfect, intricate puzzle. Components that were thought to be gratuitous at first will come back in major ways at later points in the book. Maybe it's just me, but I adore authors who blatantly show that they know exactly where they're going with</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText4963494073362155303" style="display:none">What a book! After reading this, I've come to appreciate Charles Dickens as so much more than "that guy who wrote the Christmas Carol."<br><br>One thing I love is his ability to create a perfect storyline. Everything in this book fits together in the end like a perfect, intricate puzzle. Components that were thought to be gratuitous at first will come back in major ways at later points in the book. Maybe it's just me, but I adore authors who blatantly show that they know exactly where they're going with every sentence of the story. The ending packs a serious punch, too.<br><br>The characters in this book are exceptional, as well. My personal favorite was Madame Defarge. It's probably me and my general love for "the bad guy" in stories, but I loved every scene she was in. I also like the fact the Dickens gave her a reason for hating the aristocracy so much, as compared to her husband. The wood-sawyer/roadmender was interesting, too, if only for entertainment value. But of course, I'm sure anyone going around screaming, "My little guillotine! Off with <em>her</em> head! Off his <em>his</em> head! Hahahaha!" for no apparent reason except to please the majority might interest anybody.<br><br>This book was also a strong commentary regarding the Revolution. It was interesting to see the ironic way in which Dickens compares the aristocracy to the angry revolutionaries. The revolutionaries are mad for the aristocracy hurting and killing the innocent. Then, they turn right around and start killing plenty of innocent people for the sake of watching their heads roll.<br><br>I understand this book isn't for everyone. The plot is complex, there are plenty of characters to keep track of, and it takes a long time to get exciting. But, trust me, if you stick with it, it will pay off in the end.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="4963494073362155303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_175180576" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175180576" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david" title="David"><img alt="David" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1338679848p2/3335664.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175180576?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 04, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/3335664-david" title="David">David</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/177574.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="Audio CD 9780143058151">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=british-literature">british-literature</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=french-revolution">french-revolution</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=18th-century">18th-century</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=england">england</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=france">france</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer175180576" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer8167044950349351168">This is another one of those Charles Dickens classics I was supposed to read as a kid and never did. Since I've never seen any of the movies either, it was actually pretty unspoiled for me, though I did know how it ends (anyone growing up in the English-speaking world can hardly have avoided knowing Sydney Carton's famous last lines: <em>"It is a far, far better thing that I do, than I have ever done; it is a far, far better rest that I go to than I have ever known."</em>.<br><br>Once again, I am in awe of Dicke</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText8167044950349351168" style="display:none">This is another one of those Charles Dickens classics I was supposed to read as a kid and never did. Since I've never seen any of the movies either, it was actually pretty unspoiled for me, though I did know how it ends (anyone growing up in the English-speaking world can hardly have avoided knowing Sydney Carton's famous last lines: <em>"It is a far, far better thing that I do, than I have ever done; it is a far, far better rest that I go to than I have ever known."</em>.<br><br>Once again, I am in awe of Dickens's ability to craft larger-than-life characters whose defining personality traits and conversational tics carry them strongly through the story, and his depiction of France before and during the Revolution is as vivid and bloody as the Terror, despite his exercising all the expected Victorian restraint when it comes to actually describing bloodshed. He also contrasts Paris with London, and not always in London's favor; Dickens was a marvelous social critic of his time, and with understated clarity he shows the reader how, while the British aristocracy was no longer trampling peasants beneath their horses' feet with impunity, the English court system was hardly more just or less rapacious and corrupt than the French.<br><br>The reader can be forgiven for thinking it's just a historical novel about the French Revolution and the thrilling escape of some of its would-be victims. Dickens tells us what the novel is really about in the last chapter:<br><br><blockquote>And yet there is not in France, with its rich variety of soil and climate, a blade, a leaf, a root, a sprig, a peppercorn, which will grow to maturity under conditions more certain than those that have produced this horror. Crush humanity out of shape once more, under similar hammers, and it will twist itself into the same tortured forms. Sow the same seed of rapacious license and oppression over again, and it will surely yield the same fruit according to its kind.</blockquote><br><br>The story itself is typically Dickensian in that it is full of memorable characters who are all brought onstage separately and then brought together by a tightening web of plot threads that ends up tying everyone together one way or another. Once Dickens introduces a character, he means to use that character until the very end, and will use any improbable plot device to make sure everyone is where he wants them to be. So of course the spy who is known to the Defarges is the very same man whom Sydney Carton saw tried years earlier in London; of course the nephew of the Marquis who imprisoned Doctor Manette (who once employed Monsieur Defarge) is the very same man who flees France and marries his daughter; of course Sydney Carton and Jerry Cruncher just happen to be in Paris on business (with the "man of business" Mr. Lorry) when Charles Darnay goes there, etc. And there is the most improbable plot device of all, telegraphed at the beginning of the book when Carton faces Darnay during that London trial. But it all works to create a tense and very enjoyable novel.<br><br>One of my chief complaints about Dickens (besides his overuse of coincidence) is his very Victorian view of women: always angels of one kind or another, whether fallen or still high on their pedestals. But he almost redeems himself of that in this book with his Angel of Death, Madame Defarge (and her sidekick, The Vengeance), one of the scariest ladies in British literature. And the final confrontation between Madame Defarge and Miss Pross was all the more epic for that Dickens so rarely resolves a situation with a scene of violence, and this time he did it with two bad-ass women, both of them practically waving their national flags as they went at each other.<br><br>Definitely a favorite, and one I should have read earlier.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="8167044950349351168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

<div class="review" id="review_157242383" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157242383" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5184971-danger" title="Danger"><img alt="Danger" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395870819p2/5184971.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/157242383?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jun 08, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/5184971-danger" title="Danger">Danger</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer157242383" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer2766994540865091709">About 30 pages into this book, I was struck with a moment of panic:<br><br>WHAT'S GOING ON HERE? WHERE THE HELL IS GARFIELD?!?<br><br>Had the lasagna-loving feline been uncerimoniously behead on the guillotine before the happenings of page 1? Without my favorite cartoon cat's wry, laid-back sense of wit these are surely THE WORST OF TIMES!<br><br>That is when I realized I was reading the classic text A Tale of Two CITIES, by Charles Dickens and not watching the 2006 cinematic masterpiece Garfield: A TAIL of Two KITTIES</br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText2766994540865091709" style="display:none">About 30 pages into this book, I was struck with a moment of panic:<br><br>WHAT'S GOING ON HERE? WHERE THE HELL IS GARFIELD?!?<br><br>Had the lasagna-loving feline been uncerimoniously behead on the guillotine before the happenings of page 1? Without my favorite cartoon cat's wry, laid-back sense of wit these are surely THE WORST OF TIMES!<br><br>That is when I realized I was reading the classic text A Tale of Two CITIES, by Charles Dickens and not watching the 2006 cinematic masterpiece Garfield: A TAIL of Two KITTIES! <br><br>Holy crap! How embarrassing!<br><br>Against my better judgement, I decided to keep reading, hoping that at some point Garfield would pop up and say something hilarious about hating Mondays. Well, fellow readers, he doesn't. I repeat: GARFIELD IS NOT IN THIS BOOK! AT ALL!<br><br>Instead, Dickens (is that his real name? LOL!) crafts a tale of sacrifice and redemption set against the bleak background of the French Revolution. <br><br>Overall, I guess it's an okay book, if you're into the "classics" sort-of thing, but I believe in my heart of hearts that this novel really could've benefitted by AT LEAST a cameo from Jon Arbuckle or something.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="2766994540865091709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class="review" id="review_18649438" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18649438" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad" title="Brad"><img alt="Brad" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1308747432p2/1022982.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18649438?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Mar 22, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1022982-brad" title="Brad">Brad</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=most-hated">most-hated</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=classic">classic</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18649438" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer11564841254924901749">A painful beast of a book. It took me five attempts to get past page one hundred, and when I finally did break that barrier I pressed on until the very end so that I didn't have to suffer ever again.<br><br>Dickens is a problem for me. I admit it freely.<br><br>There was a time, many years ago, when I was a fan. I read <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2623.Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations by Charles Dickens">Great Expectations</a> for the first time in grade four, and I was in love with the book and Dickens. And I imagine that some part of my social consciousness, which wasn't a gift from my parents, was</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText11564841254924901749" style="display:none">A painful beast of a book. It took me five attempts to get past page one hundred, and when I finally did break that barrier I pressed on until the very end so that I didn't have to suffer ever again.<br><br>Dickens is a problem for me. I admit it freely.<br><br>There was a time, many years ago, when I was a fan. I read <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2623.Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations by Charles Dickens">Great Expectations</a> for the first time in grade four, and I was in love with the book and Dickens. And I imagine that some part of my social consciousness, which wasn't a gift from my parents, was planted with the seeds of Dickens.<br><br>Over the years, though, Dickens and I have grown apart. <br><br>I don't mean that I have "outgrown" him in any sort of condescending manner. It's not the sort of thing I expect anyone else to do, nor is it something that I blame fully on Dickens. No, we've grown apart as many couples do when one person changes through life and experience and the other remains constant.<br><br>I have become a radical over the years, and Dickens...well, he's still as bourgeois left as ever, and we're not compatible any more. He venerates the comforts of the middle class; he expounds the virtues of law and order and charity; he attacks the indignities of the abuses of power but only offers imaginary methods for overcoming them, mythologizing the bourgeoisie's ability to overthrow the things that ail us; he vilifies those who seek more radical solutions; and, whether he admits it or not, he still believes in the superiority of nobility and noble blood.<br><br>So when he starts to attack the revolutionaries in Paris and uses it to illustrate the "superiority" of civilized English behavior, when Dickens' moral soapbox weighs heavier than his plot, I begin to tune out of his lecture, and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1953.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="A Tale of Two Cities by Charles Dickens">A Tale of Two Cities</a> makes me increasingly angry from page to page. <br><br>I recognize Dickens' talent. I still love his prose. And I get why people love this book, and maybe even why you do, kind reader, but I can't stand it (and I am finding it increasingly difficult to like any of his work anymore).<br><br>I may burn this someday. But I have fully annotated the version I own and while I can burn the words of others (it's the radical in me), my lovely inner narcissist simply can't burn words of my own (unless it is for catharsis). So <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1953.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="A Tale of Two Cities by Charles Dickens">A Tale of Two Cities</a> will likely survive on my shelf until I die, mocking me from its high perch in my office, whispering that a catharsis that may never come just may be necessary.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="11564841254924901749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8027366761667097872">6.0 stars. This was the first Charles Dickens novel I have ever read and I ABSOLUTELY LOVED IT!!! <span id="freeText8027366761667097872" style="display:none">6.0 stars. This was the first Charles Dickens novel I have ever read and I ABSOLUTELY LOVED IT!!! After reading this, I immediately decided that I would plan on reading the rest of Dickens books (hopefully one every couple of months until I get through them all. I was completely amazed by his characters who came instantly to life for me and about whose hopes and fears I found myself truly caring. Equally impressive was Dickens' plotting and overall story-telling ability which I thought were nothing short of masterful. Dickens writing conveyed the passions and turmoil of the French Revolution like few other works of literature I have come across. <br><br>In sum, in between two of the most famous first and last lines of any English novel (i.e., "It was the Best of Times...." and "It is a far, far better thing I do...") is an incredibly entertaining and moving story. HIGHEST POSSIBLE RECOMMENDATION!!!  </br></br></span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_39448334" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39448334" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em" title="Jennifer (aka EM)"><img alt="Jennifer (aka EM)" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1420125591p2/1393336.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39448334?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Nov 17, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em" title="Jennifer (aka EM)">Jennifer (aka EM)</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/76924.A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="Paperback 9780451526564">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1393336-jennifer-aka-em?shelf=for-the-desert-island">for-the-desert-island</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer39448334" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer3823784174986447908">Above all else, this is a love story. It is a story of family and friendship and loyalty. It is Dickens at his most florid and most rhetorical, his most humane, his most [melo]dramatic; yet in many ways, his most precise. I vacillate between this and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31242.Bleak_House" title="Bleak House by Charles Dickens">Bleak House</a> as my favourites of his. I would tell you, if you've not read Dickens, to start here. This is as seminal a work in English literature as <em>King Lear</em> or perhaps a more apt comparison, <em>Romeo and Juliet</em>.<br><br>For this is a love story.<br><br>The last three</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText3823784174986447908" style="display:none">Above all else, this is a love story. It is a story of family and friendship and loyalty. It is Dickens at his most florid and most rhetorical, his most humane, his most [melo]dramatic; yet in many ways, his most precise. I vacillate between this and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31242.Bleak_House" title="Bleak House by Charles Dickens">Bleak House</a> as my favourites of his. I would tell you, if you've not read Dickens, to start here. This is as seminal a work in English literature as <em>King Lear</em> or perhaps a more apt comparison, <em>Romeo and Juliet</em>.<br><br>For this is a love story.<br><br>The last three chapters are intense and so evocative they take my breath away. Beyond the suspense and drama (I'm so glad I have such a bad memory - I only vaguely remembered the book, so was able to enjoy the unfolding of the story despite knowing the general gist of the outcome), they show Dickens as the consummate story-teller that he is, and a masterful rhetorician: the stand-off between Miss Pross and Mme. Defarge is absolutely stunning in its telling and as it reveals Dickens' choices about how to tell an important part of the end - how to bring to completion the themes of loyalty, friendship and love; the positive and life-giving power of allegiance to an ideal as opposed to the destructive and death-inducing allegiance to ideology. <br><br><em>"[Mme. Defarge] knew full well that Miss Pross was the family's devoted friend; Miss Pross knew full well that Madame Defarge was the family's malevolent enemy.<br><br>It was in vain for Madame Defarge to struggle and to strike; Miss Pross, <u>with the vigorous tenacity of love, always so much stronger than hate,</u> clasped her tight, and even lifted her from the floor in the struggle that they had."</br></br></em> <br><br>(my underline - I just love that phrase: "the vigorous tenacity of love")<br><br>There is something, even, of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15997.Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost by John Milton">Paradise Lost</a> here. There is something on that grand a scale in depicting the fight between good and evil. Among so many dualities, set up from that absolutely extraordinary beginning paragraph that many of us can quote by heart and the title itself, good and evil/love and hate/life and death is what this book comes down to. <br><br>There is also an acknowledgement of the grey area between the polarities. There is an understanding that evil people are doing evil acts, but that these are fomented within a social and historical context: <em>"Crush humanity out of shape once more, under similar hammers, and it will twist itself into the same tortured forms. Sow the same seeds of rapacious licence and oppression over again, and it will surely yield the same fruit according to its kind."</em><br><br>It's very big: thematically, historically. And yet it's also very (for Dickens) concise. That combination is extremely potent, and probably the central reason I love it so much.<br><br>Something else to be said is the relative absence of humour or caricature as is common in Dickens, with the exception of Jerry Cruncher. Dickens - with, again, that precision - uses Jerry throughout the novel as a character that unites and advances the plot in specific ways at specific points; but he also allows him to evolve and grow in a way that he doesn't always, even in <em>Bleak House.</em> <br><br>I notice this time 'round that Dickens has the jaw-dropping audacity to insert what is, I believe, the only sustained comical scene in the novel (Pross and Jerry trying to make a plan; Jerry's "wows") right before its most tragic. And it IS funny, and a few pages later those tears of laughter turn to tears of anguish. This is <em>incredible</em> writing.<br><br>The dualities in <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> could be the focus of an entire review, but the duality alluded to in the title - the two cities, at two different times - allows Dickens to make a separate, more practical and equally important point. The Dickensian point. (As an aside, the cities, the years - places, times, inanimate objects (Sainte Guillotine) are personified; occupations (knitting, shoe-making, wood-cutting, road-mending) take on an importance beyond the pedestrian, become representative in a way that supports its epic feel.)  <br><br>While Miss Pross represents England and a sense of English superiority, Dickens is not merely dredging up the ages old English-French conflict; he's saying something more subtle: that London at the time he was writing was a hair's breadth away from Paris during The Terror in terms of social inequities.  That these conditions, in which human brutality and cruelty arise and dominate - for a time - are predictable, repeatable. That there is a dark side to the coin: the best of times and the worst of times; wisdom and foolishness; hope and despair exist side by side across all times, all places.  <br><br>The point of Carton's prophetic observations at the end is that this, too, shall pass; that, in the blink of an eye, positions will be switched <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(his self-sacrifice being an example; and then much can be said about Carton as a Christ-figure here) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Yet as constants, Dickens is also always the optimist: people have the capacity for great good, as well as great evil; retribution and vengeance will be matched and outlasted by generosity and goodness; and love will, in the end, triumph.<br><br>For this is, above all else, a love story. <br><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="3823784174986447908" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="xhr_comment_body">
<a href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em" style="font-weight:bold">Jennifer (aka EM)</a>
<span id="freeTextContainer14379796935520225169">It's funny you should mention that, as I was reflecting on whether, in the end, Dickens wanted us to see Mme Defarge as "evil personified." Or just as</span>
<span id="freeText14379796935520225169" style="display:none">It's funny you should mention that, as I was reflecting on whether, in the end, Dickens wanted us to see Mme Defarge as "evil personified." Or just as the inevitable outcome of brutality and cruelty on personality, and what happens when ideology trumps humanity.  <br><br>He did make it very clear, at the end, what were her reasons for behaving so ruthlessly toward the family - to wish and work for their 'extinction.' <br><br>But I think ultimately he didn't leave her with that ground to stand on to excuse her actions: esp. because he set up the "how far is too far" argument, and contrasted her with her husband - who was about to (or would, she feared) show mercy. <br><br>He put her in the same camp, and described her as even *more* extreme than Jacques 3 &amp; the Vengeance, both of whom were prepared to kill Charles, Lucie and Lucie's baby. He spent a good part of a chapter detailing her psychology - I don't have the book with me, but in modern-day parlance, she was a psychopath - no conscience, capable of no compassion, no pity and wouldn't expect any to be shown to her. I have to go and re-read - did he use the word soul? (as in, she didn't have one).<br><br>Evil is a fraught word - esp. in a novel that is drawing so heavily upon Christian imagery, speaking directly of "the resurrection and the life", and setting up two characters - Mme Defarge and Sydney Carton - as the ultimate destroyer and the ultimate saviour, respectively. But if that's what he's doing, I think the term evil with all its implications not too extreme.<br><br>Perhaps he is also saying that evil can be a man-made thing, forged (da 'farge' - ??) by social conditions. I think that interpretation not too far-fetched either.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="14379796935520225169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
